    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer6945278616833863201">In Hermann Hesse's <i>Beneath the Wheel</i> or <i>The Prodigy</i>, Hans Giebenrath lives among the dull and respectable townsfolk of a sleepy Black Forest village. Hans dutifully follows the regimen of tireless study and endless examinations, his success rewa</span>
  <span id="freeText6945278616833863201" style="display:none">In Hermann Hesse's <i>Beneath the Wheel</i> or <i>The Prodigy</i>, Hans Giebenrath lives among the dull and respectable townsfolk of a sleepy Black Forest village. When he is discovered to be an exceptionally gifted student, the entire community presses him onto a path of serious scholarship. Hans dutifully follows the regimen of tireless study and endless examinations, his success rewarded only with more crushing assignments. When Hans befriends a rebellious young poet, he begins to imagine other possibilities outside the narrowly circumscribed world of the academy. Finally sent home after a nervous breakdown, Hans is revived by nature and romance, and vows never to return to the gray conformity of the academic system.</span>

<a href="/questions/1341474-why-is-it-called-beneath-the-wheel">why is it called &quot;Beneath the wheel&quot; ?</a>
</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/57220472-luca-szabo"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/57220472-luca-szabo">Luca Szabo</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">A teacher of Hans' (the protagonist of the novel) warns Hans, when he begins to neglect his schoolwork, that he is in danger of being "crushed beneath<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">A teacher of Hans' (the protagonist of the novel) warns Hans, when he begins to neglect his schoolwork, that he is in danger of being "crushed beneath the wheel," a metaphor for saying that if Hans does not apply himself robotically to his school work, he will suffer. Of course, the main concept of the novel is realizing the value of succeeding in a school compared to that of living soulfully and poetically, is inferior.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1002345433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2330003199441710356">Vintage Hesse!<br/><br/>Sad story about an ambitious boy who slowly breaks down when facing the pressure of the adult world. While showing the catastrophic effects of a rigid educational system and of excessive parental expectations, it also hints at the responsibility of the young to get to grips with life and not just give up and drift along.<br/><br/>As important and relevant now as when it was written!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer606507222" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6750661745543399074"><b>Unterm Rad = Beneath the Wheel, Hermann Hesse</b><br/><br/>Beneath the Wheel is the story of Hans Giebenrath, a talented boy sent to a seminary in Maulbronn. His education is focused completely on increasing his knowledge, and neglects personal development. <br/><br/>**Spoiler Alert**<br/>His close friendship with Hermann Heilner, a less academically assiduous and more liberal fellow student, is a source of comfort for Hans. Heilner is expelled from the seminary, and Giebenrath is sent home after his academic performance de</span>
  <span id="freeText6750661745543399074" style="display:none"><b>Unterm Rad = Beneath the Wheel, Hermann Hesse</b><br /><br />Beneath the Wheel is the story of Hans Giebenrath, a talented boy sent to a seminary in Maulbronn. His education is focused completely on increasing his knowledge, and neglects personal development. <br /><br />**Spoiler Alert**<br />His close friendship with Hermann Heilner, a less academically assiduous and more liberal fellow student, is a source of comfort for Hans. Heilner is expelled from the seminary, and Giebenrath is sent home after his academic performance decreases in tandem with the onset of symptoms of mental illness. <br /><br />Back home, he finds coping with his situation difficult, having lost most of his childhood to scholastic study, and thus having never formed lasting personal relationships with anyone in his village. <br /><br />He is apprenticed as a blacksmith, and seems to find satisfaction in the work; it is visceral and concrete, as opposed to the intellectual abstraction of scholarly pursuit. Despite some personal fulfillment in his existence, Hans never fully adjusts to his new situation. On a pub crawl in a neighbouring village, he and his colleagues get drunk. Giebenrath leaves the group to walk home early. Later, ...<br /><br /><b>
  <i>“A soul that is ruined in the bud will frequently return to the springtime of its beginning and its promise-filled childhood, as though it could discover new hopes there and retie the broken threads of life. The shoots grow rapidly and eagerly, but it is only a sham life, that will never be a genuine tree.”</i>
</b><br /><br />تاریخ نخستسن خوانش: سال 1990میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: اعجوبه؛ نویسنده: هرمان هسه؛ مترجم: مینا بیگلری؛ ت‍ه‍ران‌: اس‍پ‍رک‌‏‫‬، 1367؛ در 241ص؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان آلمانی تبار سوئیسی - سده 20م<br /><br />هرمان هسه در این رمان، داستان پسربچه‌ ای باهوش را، بازگو می‌کند، که ذوق و استعدادش توسط مربیان، معلمان و مادر و پدرش، نادیده گرفته می‌شود.؛ این اثر حاوی انتقاداتی نسبت به شیوه‌ های تربیتی و تعلیمی در مدارس و خانواده‌ ها است؛ و برخی از منتقدان، به علت شباهت‌های شخصیت داستان، با زندگی «هسه» در کودکی، این کتاب را نوعی «زندگی‌نامه خودنوشت» دانسته ‌اند<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 24/08/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

  <span id="freeText16684932390838439974" style="display:none">If you're an educator, you need to read this book. <div id="review_28129349" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28129349" />
    <a title="Andy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/764075-andy"><img alt="Andy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602373490p2/764075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28129349?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2008</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28129349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8658474175552436766">Underrated brilliance from Hesse about an insanely hard-working student who finally cracks like an egg and has to slow down and appreciate the world around him, even if it kills him. This kind of tale gets pretty trite very easily, but Hesse's Germanic Zen outlook defines existence in a marvelous and offbeat light.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_79360009" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/79360009" />
    <a title="SJ Loria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/826429-sj-loria"><img alt="SJ Loria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574023800p2/826429.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/79360009?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="SJ Loria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="SJ Loria" href="/user/show/826429-sj-loria">SJ Loria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79360009" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11831387592112040064">This story showcases Hesse’s brilliant prose mixed with his thoughts on life, aging, and love. In a story centered around a bright, gifted, yet timid boy Hesse shows both sides of the spectrum, celebrating the pain and sorrow along with the joys and surprises of life. The boy’s tale is equally beautiful and tragic. He has extraordinary gifts that simply do not fit into the demanded “one narrow path” of life for small town German students at the time. Unfortunately, he is crushed beneath the whee</span>
  <span id="freeText11831387592112040064" style="display:none">This story showcases Hesse’s brilliant prose mixed with his thoughts on life, aging, and love. In a story centered around a bright, gifted, yet timid boy Hesse shows both sides of the spectrum, celebrating the pain and sorrow along with the joys and surprises of life. The boy’s tale is equally beautiful and tragic. He has extraordinary gifts that simply do not fit into the demanded “one narrow path” of life for small town German students at the time. Unfortunately, he is crushed beneath the wheel like a cog in the machine, and the world does not benefit from his talents and vision. This boy was called to join in the higher realm of thinkers and people, yet due to the conditions of society he is unable to live this out. Perhaps a line where Hesse describes learning sums up the view of society and life just as well, “Every day true scholarship seemed more beautiful, more difficult, more worthwhile.” In this novel Hesse shows life as beautiful, difficult, and worthwhile if one can overcome the obstacles presented by a society intent on suffocating dreams. The clear menace in this tale is the system of school, organized religion, and people who place their demands on students instead of letting them explore the world. Our protagonist is the victim, but the intended effect is that the reader take away this lesson and not join their ranks. Hesse still celebrates life and the poetry of every moment, and he viciously attacks those who suppress it in this tragic tale.<br />One question I had while reading this novel is whether or not adulthood itself is another enemy. The transition from childhood to adulthood is performed though love, which Hans experiences first through friendship, then through a hint of homosexuality, and then finally through heterosexual longing. The transition into adulthood opens the door to pain and suffering. Though love is beautiful, it is not clear in this novel whether or not Hesse thinks its beauty is worth the price of innocence lost. The passages describing Hans falling in love are absolutely stunning, however the cost of love remains unsettled. Is adulthood and love worth the pain of growing up? Hesse leaves this unanswered, which is a surprise in this novel. Another surprise is the passage about a homosexual experience and an unlikely person at the end of the book to accept any of the blame. There are questions open to consideration through a tale that describes the beauty of life through poetic prose. A damn good book, classic Hesse.<br /><br /><br />Quotes<br />It was possible to live in this town and give the appearance of being educated without knowing the speeches in Zarathustra. 5<br />Thus his future was mapped out, for in all of Swabia there existed but one narrow path for talented boys – that is, unless their parents were wealthy. 5<br />In a single hour he introduced Hans to an entirely new approach to learning and reading. Hands received an intimation of what tasks and puzzles lay hidden in each line and word, how thousands of scholars and investigators had expended their efforts since the earliest times to unravel these questions, and it seemed to him that he was being accepted into the ranks of these truth-seekers this very hour. 47<br />Every day true scholarship seemed more beautiful, more difficult, more worthwhile. 47<br />It has occurred to many people while they stood on this square that it would be just the right place for the good life and happiness, for something lively and gratifying to grow, for mature and good people to think glad thoughts and produce beautiful, cheerful works. 57<br />Proud and praiseworthy feelings and high hopes swelled in their breasts and it did not occur to a single one of them that this day he was selling his child for a financial advantage…it did not occur to any of the boys, nor to their fathers, that all this would perhaps not really be free. 65<br />“He realized that there are certain sins and omissions beyond forgiveness and repentance and it seemed to him that the stretcher bore not the tailor’s little son but Heilner, who now took all the pain and anger caused by Han’s faithlessness with him far into another world where people were judged not by their grades and examination marks and scholastic success but soley in accord with the purity or impurity of their consciences…they realized for a fleeting moment how irrecoverable and unique is each life and youth. 94<br />Anyone with a touch of genius seems to his teachers a freak from the very first…[a teacher’s:] task is not to produce extravagant intellects but good Latinists, arithmeticians and sober decent folk…among true geniuses the wounds almost always heal. As their personalities develop, they create their art in spite of school…thus the struggle between rule and spirit repeats itself year after year from school to school. 100<br />He was like someone in love for the first time: he felt capable of performing great heroic deed but not the daily chore of boring, petty work. 103<br />Their faces displayed the whole spectrum of shadings between vanishing childishness and budding manhood. 106<br />Hans’ thoughts and dreams now moved in this world to which he had been so long a stranger. He sought refuge from his great disappointment and hopelessness in a past that had been good to him. In those days he had been full of hope, had seen the world lying before him like a vast enchanted forest holding gruesome dangers, accursed treasures and emerald castles in its impenetrable depths. He had entered a little way into this wilderness but he had become weary before he had found miracles. 138<br />He stared at her with a mixture of unfamiliar desire and bad conscience. In this hour something broke inside him and a new, alien but enticing land with distant blue shores opened up before his soul. He did not know or could only guess what the apprehension and sweet agony signified, and did not know which was stronger, pain or desire.<br />But the desire signified…the first intimation of the mighty forces of life, and the pain signified that the morning peace had been broken, that his soul had left that childhood land which can never be found again…He must find his own way and be his own savior. 150<br />Fearful but shaken to the roots of his being, he felt the nearness of a great mystery, not knowing whether it would be delicious or dreadful but having a foretaste of both. 153<br />The pain and anger over this loss and the restlessness of his inflamed but unsatisfied passion came together in a single, agonizing confusion…In this way he discovered – perhaps too soon – his share of the secret of love, and it contained little sweetness and much bitterness. 165<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11831387592112040064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth" href="/user/show/4351439-seth">Seth</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer11061062199901767321">An English translation of my review can be found below.<br/><br/>Dieser Roman erinnerte mich daran, was ich schon in meiner Jugend an Hermann Hesse so verlockend fand.   <span id="freeText11061062199901767321" style="display:none">An English translation of my review can be found below.<br /><br />Dieser Roman erinnerte mich daran, was ich schon in meiner Jugend an Hermann Hesse so verlockend fand. Er war ein wunderbarer Schriftsteller! Seine präzisen Beschreibungen sowohl des Innenlebens des unglücklichen Klosterschülers Hans Giebenrath wie der widersprüchlichen Schönheit der physischen Umwelt, in der er zu Grunde ging, sind außerordentlich. Die wunderschöne Sprache des Romans übt eine fast magische Wirkung auf den Leser aus. <br /><br />„Unterm Rad“ ist autobiografisch in dem Sinne, daß Hesse sich selbst in Hermann Heilner, dem besten Freund von Hans, darstellt. Hermann, ein sehr begabter aber aufrührerischer junger Mensch, kommt in der repressiven Schule nicht zurecht. Da er es prinzipiell ablehnt, sich der Gesellschaftsordnung anzupassen, wird er entlassen.<br /><br />Hans wiederum verkörpert Hesses gleichnamigen Bruder, der wegen seiner eigenen Qualen Selbstmord begangen hat, nachdem die Schule ihn psychisch gebrochen hat. Im Roman wird Hans ebenfalls entlassen, aber hauptsächlich wegen seiner schlechten Gesundheit (schwindelerregende Kopfschmerzen), obwohl der Rektor es ihm auch übelnimmt, daß er unter den Einfluss von Hermann geraten ist. Aus beiden Gründen kann er dem Leistungsdruck nicht standhalten. <br /><br />Das Kloster Maulbronn, wo Hesse nach dem bestandenen Landexamen Latein gelernt hat und wo der Roman sich abspielt, wurde von der UNESCO als Weltkulturerbe anerkannt.<br /><br />Vor diesem Hintergrund ist es leicht nachzuvollziehen, wie Hesse in dem Roman das Bedürfnis hatte, sich mit der autoritären Erziehung in Deutschland des frühen zwanzigsten Jahrhunderts so schonungslos auseinanderzusetzen. Seine Bildungskritik ist überzeugend.<br /><br />Lieblingszitat: „Nur nicht matt werden, sonst kommt man unters Rad.“<br /><br />----------------------<br /><br />This novel reminded me what I found so compelling about Hermann Hesse in my youth. He was a wonderful writer! His precise descriptions of both the inner life of the unhappy cloister student Hans Giebenrath and the contradictory beauty of the physical environment, in which he perished, are extraordinary. The wonderfully beautiful language of the novel has an almost magical effect on the reader.<br /><br />“Under the Wheel” is autobiographical in the sense that Hesse represents himself in Hermann Heilner, Hans’s best friend. Hermann, a very gifted but rebellious young man, cannot cope with the repressive school. Since he refuses as a matter of principle to conform to the social order, he is expelled. <br /><br />Hans, in turn, personifies Hesse’s eponymous brother, who committed suicide because of his own torments after school destroyed him psychologically. In the novel, Hans is likewise expelled, but primarily due to his poor health (dizziness-inducing headaches), although the headmaster also resents the fact that he has fallen under Hermann’s influence. For both reasons, he cannot handle the pressure to perform. <br /><br />UNESCO has recognized the Maulbronn Cloister, where Hesse studied Latin after passing the state examination and where the novel is set, as a World Heritage Site.<br /><br />Against this backdrop, it is easy to understand why Hesse had the need in the novel to deal with the authoritarian style of schooling in Germany in the early 20th century so unsparingly. His critique of the educational system is convincing.<br /> <br />Favorite quote: “Just don’t wear yourself out, otherwise you’ll end up under the wheel.”</span>

<div id="review_3214773361" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3214773361" />
    <a title="Regina Andreassen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6417302-regina-andreassen"><img alt="Regina Andreassen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342328525p2/6417302.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3214773361?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Regina Andreassen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Regina Andreassen" href="/user/show/6417302-regina-andreassen">Regina Andreassen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780720611748" href="/book/show/191353.The_Prodigy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3214773361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer282608749661851720">3.5 stars - The Prodigy, also known as Beneath The Wheel, is a book that scrutinises youth, the construction of the Self, and the challenges of life which may result in mental health issues, ergo, the book goes beyond a mere exploration or critique of the education system. Hesse’s prose is rich, lyric, and evocative, that alone deserves the first 3 stars. <br/><br/>The first chapters of the The Prodigy are strong; I especially enjoyed chapters two, three, and four, they felt real and they were elegantly a</span>
  <span id="freeText282608749661851720" style="display:none">3.5 stars - The Prodigy, also known as Beneath The Wheel, is a book that scrutinises youth, the construction of the Self, and the challenges of life which may result in mental health issues, ergo, the book goes beyond a mere exploration or critique of the education system. Hesse’s prose is rich, lyric, and evocative, that alone deserves the first 3 stars. <br /><br />The first chapters of the The Prodigy are strong; I especially enjoyed chapters two, three, and four, they felt real and they were elegantly and effectively written and also transmit great sentiment; alas, after Hans leaves the seminar and goes back to his town the story fails to keep me engaged for it is underdeveloped. I am of the opinion that there should have been two more chapters at the seminar for, besides Hans, our protagonist, there was not enough character development. Heilner, for instance, could have been a great character , yet he is not layered. I suspect that Hermann Heilner was meant to represent the rebellious kid we often find at schools, but, is that all there should be? What else is there about Heilner that makes him an individual so unique as to be admired and deeply esteemed by Hans? Hans neglects his own need for space and his scholarly ambitions partly due to his relationship with Heilner, which he places above all; yet, Heilner, does not reciprocate to the same extent. What does having Heilner in his life reveal to Hans that makes his focus change so abruptly? I found Hans’s change in attitude a bit abrupt and inconsistent with his character. This theme needed further exploration. <br /><br />We are told that Heilner is a genius but we never really know why. The children at the seminar were there because they had brilliant minds, however, the writing never offers a proof of it besides stating that the seminar was a school for privileged intellects. Further, based on the way the story unfolds in the seminar this could have been any other school in Germany; yet, the seminar was not meant to be ‘any school’ but an elite institution; and therein lies the issue with the narrative. We don’t see, as readers, what is so special about the seminar that confers it it’s great status.<br /><br />In the The Prodigy, we know most characters superficially; some of them were too stereotypical. I prefer more nuanced characters and I did not find them in this book. I did adore Hans though and I would have liked to learn of these two characters: Flaig and Lucius.<br /><br />After chapter five ends, the prose continues to be delightfully descriptive, yet that alone was not enough to keep me as invested as I was in previous chapters. The Prodigy has much potential, nonetheless, most of what happens after chapter five was unconvincing and falls flat. In fact, I would have preferred for the book to finish by the end of chapter 5 (which would have made it a novella), rather than to have all the chapters it did and have that contrived and tedious last chapter. The denouement was disappointing not because of the final event but because of how we got ‘there’.<br /><br />The problem I see is that, Hesse seems to have lost inspiration precisely at the stage in which the story should have peaked; ergo, the story never fully blossoms. The book has some elements that hint that, by the time Hesse wrote this book, he had a rather manichaean view of the education system. <br /><br />Overall, this is a good book but not a ‘must-read’. If you like Hermann Hesse then I do recommend The Prodigy but keep in mind that Hesse’s Narcissus and Goldman is a superior book, thus more memorable. Do not expect The Prodigy to achieve Narcissus and Goldman’s status.</span>
  <a data-text-id="282608749661851720" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_167604192" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/167604192" />
    <a title="Erik Graff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff"><img alt="Erik Graff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299888977p2/974210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/167604192?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik Graff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik Graff" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff">Erik Graff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553058598" href="/book/show/25909.Beneath_the_Wheel">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Hesse fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Erik by: </span>
      no one
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/974210-erik-graff?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer167604192" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6895026118446394401">This is only Hesse's second novel and not his best. It is somewhat autobiographical and, like many of the author's novels, is a tale of a young man's disillusionment, rebellion and self-discovery. My notes on the book from the time of reading summarize it as follows: "Raised without a mother and crushed by the ordered, competitive, masculine world's expectations. The destruction of a young spirit."</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_450575343" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/450575343" />
    <a title="Bennard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7278089-bennard"><img alt="Bennard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445948498p2/7278089.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/450575343?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bennard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bennard" href="/user/show/7278089-bennard">Bennard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer450575343" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5142214480263907826">from <a href='http://www.thebookhooligan.wordpress.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Book Hooligan</a><br/><br/><i>"For he was aware that in the academy he would have to be even more ambitious if he wanted to outstrip his new fellow students. Why did he want to surpass them actually? He didn't really know himself." - Narrator</i><br/><br/>I was raised believing that education and blind respect for authority are two of the most important things that I child should value. I studied for the sake of moving on to the next level of education with a good school. At that time, academic growth was more valued</span>
  <span id="freeText5142214480263907826" style="display:none">from <a href="http://www.thebookhooligan.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book Hooligan</a><br /><br /><i>"For he was aware that in the academy he would have to be even more ambitious if he wanted to outstrip his new fellow students. Why did he want to surpass them actually? He didn't really know himself." - Narrator</i><br /><br />I was raised believing that education and blind respect for authority are two of the most important things that I child should value. I studied for the sake of moving on to the next level of education with a good school. At that time, academic growth was more valued over personal and emotional growth. However, at some point in my high school life, I rebelled. I started reading non-required literature and started paying more attention to the humanities rather than the sciences (I was in a science high school). After that, I started to value my personal and emotional growth over blind and directionless academic growth.<br /><br />Hermann Hesse's Beneath the Wheel deals with the same problem in the children of his time who were taught to value an education that only focuses on academic performance and not on the student's capability to philosophize and think by himself. It tells the story of Hans Geibenrath, a boy of genius intellect that was sent to a seminary in order to study. All of Han's life was spent studying and his reward for doing so was further study. All of his life, he has never participated much in childhood activities when his town has decided the path that he should take. When other boys in his town were fishing or swimming, he was studying hard. The result was he lost his childhood at such an early age and that he finds that he cannot properly adapt to his situation. However, he does find joy in feeling that he is superior over his other classmates because he knows that his future is secure while theirs isn't.<br /><br />During his stay in the seminary, he was a model student in the running for the top spot in the class. Hans is friendless in his new school until he meets Heilner, a rebellious romantic, and their friendship takes a toll in Hans' studies. Heilner, because of his rebellious nature, is expelled and Hans is sent back home because his studies has taken a turn for the worse as he was diagnosed with mental sickness. Hans then becomes an apprentice blacksmith and takes pride in his difficult work and he also experienced the joy and pains of love. However, the story ends in tragedy, as Hans gets drunk, falls into a river, and dies.<br /><br />The novel has a pastoral air as it Hesse describes the countryside with beautiful precision. The German countryside feels so alive under Hesse's writing and you can really see the juxtaposition between the busy life of Hans and the peaceful countryside surrounding him as if saying that the beauty of nature is something that Hans will never enjoy to the fullest because of the regiment that he is in. Any of the short time that he spends with nature quickly dissipates when Hans' elders forces him to continue with his studies. Only the rebellious Heilner has provided him with the reprieve that Hans deserves but even that has ended tragically.<br /><br />The novel may be a comment on how society values an education that concentrates solely on academic achievement. Memorizing equations, dates, and facts without really pursuing the greater meaning of everything that they study in order for the students to better themselves emotionally. A life of study in order to have access to further study seems pointless. Life, as the countryside seems to imply in the book, is meant to be enjoyed yet the authorities in the novel are forcing young people like Hans to forget about life and instead sacrifice themselves to the blind pursuit of the academe. In the end life is wasted just because children with so much potential like Hans are pressured in doing something that they don't like.<br /><br />Death is also a central theme of the novel. Death is uncertain and does not play favorites. Genius or not, death will take you when the time comes and it will be too late for you to realize whether you've lived life to the fullest or not. One of the most moving parts of the book was when a classmate of Hans died without making any friends because of his studies and when the father of the departed asked the class about who is closest to his son and nobody came forward, the father broke down. Maybe because the father realized that he led his son to a life of pure intellect without any semblance of friendship and, at the death of his son, it is already too late to start. Death also took Hans in the end and his potential was never realized and he lived a very unhappy life with only a few happy moments in between.<br /><br />However, I can only rate this novel three stars because the narrative was tedious. The narrator was always going into monologues that should be more appropriate in philosophical treatises and not novels. And the way that the narrator describes the German countryside, although beautiful in its prose, is overwrought and unnecessary. This may be one of the few novels that I read where my main complaint was because of the third-person omniscient narrator.<br /><br />All in all, the novel was a solid piece considering the fact that it is the first novel that I read from Herman Hesse. It may be tedious to read a novel that is filled with spiritual and philosophical monologues of the narrator while not moving the plot forward but it was also filled with such poignancy and tragedy that can totally move you. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5142214480263907826" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_39813874" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39813874" />
    <a title="Patrick Gibson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1758594-patrick-gibson"><img alt="Patrick Gibson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228068580p2/1758594.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39813874?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick Gibson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick Gibson" href="/user/show/1758594-patrick-gibson">Patrick Gibson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1758594-patrick-gibson?shelf=contemporary-literature">contemporary-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39813874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11738510352622891885">I am from the ‘sturm und drang’ school of life. I can only read a Hesse novel with Mahler playing in the background. The more angst, the better. Give me irrational anguish, sorrow, torment. <br/>In college, I carried Hesse novels under my arm the way super-Christians carry their Bibles like five day pads. Agony and gloom where never far. And I was of the opinion Hans Giebenrath was my autobiography. Contemporaries were raging against the system screamed copies of ‘Catcher in the Rye,’ while I opted f</span>
  <span id="freeText11738510352622891885" style="display:none">I am from the ‘sturm und drang’ school of life. I can only read a Hesse novel with Mahler playing in the background. The more angst, the better. Give me irrational anguish, sorrow, torment. <br />In college, I carried Hesse novels under my arm the way super-Christians carry their Bibles like five day pads. Agony and gloom where never far. And I was of the opinion Hans Giebenrath was my autobiography. Contemporaries were raging against the system screamed copies of ‘Catcher in the Rye,’ while I opted for a pseudo-European, post-Wagner leitmotif singing the ballad of ‘Beneath the Wheel.’ <br />Of course, I wasn’t really a brilliant child ground and crushed by adults fostering an educational system designed to pound the spirit and vanquish imagination. Hans, the protagonist in this novel, faced no greater challenge to his soul than any student today. It was (and is) the beauty of the prose that shattered my world. A product of comic books and forced curriculum, this post-romantic epistle was one of the first novels to grab me by the spleen and twist.<br /><br />I am re-reading the Hesse novels now as (sort of) an adult. I wonder if I will see what I saw then. I know ‘Beneath the Wheel’ is Hesse’s first published book and a primer for the increasing complexity of the later works. The struggle with the dark side personified by close friendships is better expressed in ‘Narcissus and Goldman,’ but for a treatise on themes of antiestablishment, personal freedom and the struggle all men face in their path through life to be themselves in the face of society pressures, this is an excellent primer. To conform rather than being true to oneself is the basis for many of Hesse’s books. The fact that the Nazi’s picked his books to burn is a good signal to ‘read’ and ‘keep reading.’<br /><br />“At this very instant the boy who was the object of all these threats was drifting slowly down the cool river. All nausea, shame and suffering had passed from him; the cold bluish autumn night looked down on the dark shape of his drifting body and the dark water played with his hands and hair and bloodless lips. No one saw him floating downstream, except perhaps an otter setting out even before daybreak for the hunt, eying him cagily as he glided past without a sound. No one knew how he came to be in the water. Perhaps the sight of the beautiful water had attracted him and when he bent over the water the night and the pale moon had seemed so peaceful and restful that weariness and fear drove him with quiet inevitability into the shadow of death.”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11738510352622891885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2094520259" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2094520259" />
    <a title="Maja" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53101848-maja"><img alt="Maja" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490719890p2/53101848.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2094520259?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maja" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maja" href="/user/show/53101848-maja">Maja</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783518365526" href="/book/show/137081.Unterm_Rad">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53101848-maja?shelf=age-adult">age-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53101848-maja?shelf=genre-classics">genre-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53101848-maja?shelf=topic-mental-health">topic-mental-health</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2094520259" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3368082112573972249"><i>A soul that is ruined in the bud will frequently return to the springtime of its beginning and its promise-filled childhood, as though it could discover new hopes there and retie the broken threads of life. The shoots grow rapidly and eagerly, but it is only a sham life that will never be a genuine tree.</i><br/><br/>Wow. This book really touched something within my soul. You know the feel when you read something and it just resonates with you and it makes you <b>feel</b> things? Yeah. That was this book for me.<br/><br/>With</span>
  <span id="freeText3368082112573972249" style="display:none"><i>A soul that is ruined in the bud will frequently return to the springtime of its beginning and its promise-filled childhood, as though it could discover new hopes there and retie the broken threads of life. The shoots grow rapidly and eagerly, but it is only a sham life that will never be a genuine tree.</i><br /><br />Wow. This book really touched something within my soul. You know the feel when you read something and it just resonates with you and it makes you <b>feel</b> things? Yeah. That was this book for me.<br /><br />Without spoiling much I can say that this is a sad story about a young, very talented and intelligent boy who perishes by the strict routine his father and his teachers enforce on him, never allowing him even a moment of rest, pushing him to always be the best and aspire to nothing else than that, ultimately overstraining his nerves and ruining his mental health. While the school system has improved a lot since Hesse's time, I often feel as though some people still could use a reminder that school and grades aren't everything that matters in life, that in fact a student's mental health is more important, and that if you don't take that in the consideration, you could not be doing them a favour despite your best intentions. Because nobody wanted to hurt Hans in this story. When in the end, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[after his funeral <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, the cobbler mentions to his father and his teachers that they may be at least partially at fault for the boy's situation, they don't want to believe it. They never meant to hurt him, they tried to help him in what they thought was the best way they could. Yet it ended so horribly.<br /><br />All in all, this was a very interesting book, and definitely an emotional read that will probably stay with me for a long time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3368082112573972249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2893061277" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2893061277" />
    <a title="Bohdan Pechenyak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13542582-bohdan-pechenyak"><img alt="Bohdan Pechenyak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520453591p2/13542582.jpg" /></a>

        <a title="Bohdan Pechenyak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bohdan Pechenyak" href="/user/show/13542582-bohdan-pechenyak">Bohdan Pechenyak</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2893061277" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4810796195672846097">One of the earlier Hesse’s novels, touching on his usual themes of academic ambition, life of the mind, artistic vision, the oppressive social system, including the academia, the employment, the cultural mores etc. It is quite easy to get caught beneath the heavy ever-turning wheel of the system, especially for a sensitive spirit unprepared to the practical vagaries of life. Not Hesse’s best or most gripping, but a clear stepping stone toward later novels. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1931488202" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1931488202" />
    <a title="Kelsey Dangelo-Worth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16513891-kelsey-dangelo-worth"><img alt="Kelsey Dangelo-Worth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1358384336p2/16513891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1931488202?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelsey Dangelo-Worth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelsey Dangelo-Worth" href="/user/show/16513891-kelsey-dangelo-worth">Kelsey Dangelo-Worth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1931488202" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3668300661689845731">Teachers are cruel assholes that only seek to destroy the individuality, art, and childhood of their students and destroy all the poetry of the books they teach. <br/><br/>It’s No Child Left Behind, the Bildungsroman/Incredibly Tragic Novel. <br/><br/>So, me, an English teacher of little bildungsroman adolescents moving from childhood to adulthood, in the present time where we are all embracing of individuality and art, a nature lover and a lover of small town life, I at once agree with the beauty and message of th</span>
  <span id="freeText3668300661689845731" style="display:none">Teachers are cruel assholes that only seek to destroy the individuality, art, and childhood of their students and destroy all the poetry of the books they teach. <br /><br />It’s No Child Left Behind, the Bildungsroman/Incredibly Tragic Novel. <br /><br />So, me, an English teacher of little bildungsroman adolescents moving from childhood to adulthood, in the present time where we are all embracing of individuality and art, a nature lover and a lover of small town life, I at once agree with the beauty and message of this book and also felt like I was being hit over the head with it repeatedly while struggling through the melodramatic slog.<br /><br />This book and I got off on the wrong foot right away with its “these country people are all Philistines” tone, and when Hans (the tortured boy genius who works his youth away to be the best at school and who is overworked by his teachers), burnt out and sickly and dying, flunks out of school and falls in love with and over idealizes the common people. I’m not sure which one is worse. Everything in the book is black and white, perfect and evil. It's dreamlike and fairytale like, but the narrative voice and the slog through it all is tedious and overblown.<br /><br />I like the message: let kids be kids. Let kids enjoy childhood, let them play and grow and enjoy life and find out who they are. Don’t be a snob, don’t destroy poetry for what it is (the art of capturing the soul and the world—shown beautifully in the poetry of this book). Great message, though, maybe it was preaching to the choir? Maybe the characters felt too delicate and unreal? Maybe there wasn't enough poetry? Maybe it was too predictable? Too melodramatic? Something just didn't work for me.<br /><br />The book is beautifully written, has a true eye for people and character and description, for sentiment. But, when I finished, I just didn’t feel like I was left with anything. No complexity, nothing to ponder or wonder or challenge my thinking, no character (except perhaps the rebellious, angry poet kid) or scene or anything that stayed with me. It seemed trite. <br /><br />I was warned not to judge Hesse based on this book. He’s considered a genius. I’m not quite seeing it here. I was left with: meh, ok. Grade: B- <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3668300661689845731" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_12569609" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12569609" />
    <a title="Malcolm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/783966-malcolm"><img alt="Malcolm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1200369526p2/783966.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12569609?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Malcolm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Malcolm" href="/user/show/783966-malcolm">Malcolm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Seekers, </span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12569609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10878685722613178717"> For those not familiar with the works of Hermann Hesse, you might do no better than starting off with this book – easily read in a day this book sets out themes to be pursued further in other works by the author. <br/><br/> Essentially, Beneath the Wheel is the story of a brilliant child, Hans Giebenrath, who finds himself ground and crushed by the expectations of the adults and an educational system that thinks that if an ounce of Homer is good, then a pound will be all the better – even during ones sum</span>
  <span id="freeText10878685722613178717" style="display:none"> For those not familiar with the works of Hermann Hesse, you might do no better than starting off with this book – easily read in a day this book sets out themes to be pursued further in other works by the author. <br /><br /> Essentially, Beneath the Wheel is the story of a brilliant child, Hans Giebenrath, who finds himself ground and crushed by the expectations of the adults and an educational system that thinks that if an ounce of Homer is good, then a pound will be all the better – even during ones summer vacation in lieu of swimming and fishing. The book details Han’s short path of life, like the arc of an arrow shot into the sky, from the heights of academia, his friendship with a student in the private residential monastery school who refuses to be bent by the system and is ultimately expelled, and the fall of the arrow to the ground with Han’s own academic and personal immobilization and collapse. <br /><br /> This theme is repeated by Hesse, with modifications and complications, in his other works including The Prodigy and Demian. The struggle of the individual with the dark side personified by close friendships with very peers either of the light or darker side, is further pursued in his works such as Narcissus and Goldmund and Steppenwolf. <br /><br /> Hermann Hesse was certainly one of the preeminent writers of the 20th century and the fact his works were centered out for burning by the Nazi’s in the streets ofBerlin is reason enough to have a look. Themes of anti-establishment, personal freedom, and the struggle all men face in their path through life to be themselves in the face of societal, family and other pressures, including intra-psychic ones, to conform rather than being true to oneself provide ample grist for many of his books. <br /><br /> Malcolm Watts BA MSW 2006<br /><br /> Malcolm Wstts is a novelist, writer, photographer and reivewer. Visit his website <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.authorsden.com/malcolmwatts">www.authorsden.com/malcolmwatts</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10878685722613178717" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1936440111" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1936440111" />
    <a title="reem" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7253062-reem"><img alt="reem" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1538065503p2/7253062.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1936440111?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="reem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="reem" href="/user/show/7253062-reem">reem</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1936440111" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4985804832434963111"><i>"As far as teachers are concerned, they define young geniuses as those who are bad, disrespectful, smoke at fourteen, fall in love at fifteen, can be found at sixteen hanging out in bars, read forbidden books, write scandalous essays, occasionally stare down a teacher in class, are marked in the attendance book as rebels, and are budding candidates for room-arrest."</i> <br/><br/>I've said it before and I'll say it again: stop tormenting kids with education. In this story a young, brilliant boy, who for the b</span>
  <span id="freeText4985804832434963111" style="display:none"><i>"As far as teachers are concerned, they define young geniuses as those who are bad, disrespectful, smoke at fourteen, fall in love at fifteen, can be found at sixteen hanging out in bars, read forbidden books, write scandalous essays, occasionally stare down a teacher in class, are marked in the attendance book as rebels, and are budding candidates for room-arrest."</i> <br /><br />I've said it before and I'll say it again: stop tormenting kids with education. In this story a young, brilliant boy, who for the better part of the book did nothing but study because that's what his father, teachers and townspeople thought he should do with his time, moved from a point of pleasing and accepting naivete to heart-wrenching madness. I became aggravated half-way through the book because of the way everyone was pressuring him to do better, be better, never stop, and never once considered that what the boy needed was a little space to develop and breathe. When Hans begins to lose it in boarding school (aka <i>falling beneath the wheel</i>), the doctor laughs it off as "nervousness" and says he wouldn't be surprised if the boy starts showing symptoms of St. Vitus Dance.<br /><br />I don't really know if I enjoyed the book, I mean, it was okay at times and too simple to be taken seriously at others. I think I got the message Hesse was attempting to deliver - I sympathized with the kids and failed to fathom what drove the adults to act so stupidly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4985804832434963111" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_76623055" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76623055" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76623055?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katie" href="/user/show/1916520-katie">Katie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76623055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer697256660795151255">This book is Hesse's indictment of educational systems that value memorization and dissection over appreciation of beauty, that convey knowledge without provoking understanding. Students are working, working, working, but never getting anywhere worthwhile. In my favorite passage, student/poet Hermann Heilner articulates the failures of his highly respected and competitive school:<br/><br/>"We're reading Homer...as though the <i>Odyssey</i> were a cookbook. Two verses an hour and then the whole thing is masticate</span>
  <span id="freeText697256660795151255" style="display:none">This book is Hesse's indictment of educational systems that value memorization and dissection over appreciation of beauty, that convey knowledge without provoking understanding. Students are working, working, working, but never getting anywhere worthwhile. In my favorite passage, student/poet Hermann Heilner articulates the failures of his highly respected and competitive school:<br /><br />"We're reading Homer...as though the <i>Odyssey</i> were a cookbook. Two verses an hour and then the whole thing is masticated word by word and inspected until you're ready to throw up. But at the end of the hour the professor will say: 'Notice how nicely the poet has turned this phrase! This has afforded you an insight into the secret of poetic creativity!' Just like a little icing around the aorists and particles so you won't choke on them completely."<br /><br />I had art history professors who taught like that--thirty minutes analyzing a painting's iconography, "isn't it beautiful," next slide.<br /><br />This novel is spot on, though depressing. I can see Hesse's ideas about duality/moderation developing in this early book. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2951866555" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2951866555" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5258058-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490047542p2/5258058.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2951866555?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/5258058-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10553815-beneath-the-wheel">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2951866555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer28291198152149227">For a while, I thought this book would be one of my favorites of the year. Hesse describing the experiences of Hans at school are truly great and cause one to question the educational approaches of some schools. However, the final 1/3 of the book that follows Hans post-school adventures drags. Perhaps Hesse was going for a deeper meaning with his death by drowning, but given that it happens in the final pages long after he has left school, it feels jarring and out of place more than had he taken</span>
  <span id="freeText28291198152149227" style="display:none">For a while, I thought this book would be one of my favorites of the year. Hesse describing the experiences of Hans at school are truly great and cause one to question the educational approaches of some schools. However, the final 1/3 of the book that follows Hans post-school adventures drags. Perhaps Hesse was going for a deeper meaning with his death by drowning, but given that it happens in the final pages long after he has left school, it feels jarring and out of place more than had he taken his life definitively as he contemplates briefly after leaving school. It was also quite a struggle to feel any kind of empathy for Hans when Emma abruptly disappears and suddenly, despite him being less than warm towards her, is angered that she left without a real goodbye. Of course, it is what one would expect from a teenager, but it feels like something ripped out of a YA novel in an otherwise more adult book. <br /><br />Overall, not a terrible read, but I think Hesse should have either strengthened the final 1/3 or made it a novella. </span>
  <a data-text-id="28291198152149227" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_859401096" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/859401096" />
    <a title="Brandon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20695588-brandon"><img alt="Brandon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579729499p2/20695588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/859401096?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brandon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brandon" href="/user/show/20695588-brandon">Brandon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553058598" href="/book/show/25909.Beneath_the_Wheel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20695588-brandon?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20695588-brandon?shelf=bildungsroman">bildungsroman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20695588-brandon?shelf=selfhood">selfhood</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20695588-brandon?shelf=manhood">manhood</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer859401096" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9745755859030841195">"When a tree is polled, it will sprout new shoots nearer its roots. A soul that is ruined in the bud will frequently return to the springtime of its beginnings and its promise-filled childhood, as though it could discover new hopes there and retie the broken threads of life. The shoots grow rapidly and eagerly, but it is only a sham life that will never be a genuine tree." (151)<br/><br/>****<br/><br/>Fuuuuuuuuck, re-reading this and I'm feeling like I was so absorbed by it way back when because I was in danger of </span>
  <span id="freeText9745755859030841195" style="display:none">"When a tree is polled, it will sprout new shoots nearer its roots. A soul that is ruined in the bud will frequently return to the springtime of its beginnings and its promise-filled childhood, as though it could discover new hopes there and retie the broken threads of life. The shoots grow rapidly and eagerly, but it is only a sham life that will never be a genuine tree." (151)<br /><br />****<br /><br />Fuuuuuuuuck, re-reading this and I'm feeling like I was so absorbed by it way back when because I was in danger of being "dragged beneath the wheel" (116). And I eventually was, so, yay! Interestingly, did I not first read this the summer after I myself slacked in my attendance in a Hebrew class? Nice. Did I not empathize more with Heilner? I believe so. Time to get mad at the world and write poems about dead leaves! As per the headmaster's description, "He is a restless, dissatisfied fellow' he may be gifted, but he does not accomplish much and does not have a good influence on you" (117). Squandering one's gifts is a perfect reaction to the catastrophe of Modernity. Am I sitting here typing nonsense because I'm high off my own fucking perfume? Yes. I failed myself, having typed "catastrophe"; I envisioned a gray mess of needles and steel wire but could not form this into a single word to convey my feeling. Thistles. "The latter's previous melancholy had changed into a restless and biting intellectuality which criticized the monastery teachers, companions, the weather, human life in general and the existence of God, but occasionally would also lead to cantankerousness or silly impulsive pranks" (120).<br /><br />There is a rather no-fun-allowed New Yorker article (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2018/11/19/hermann-hesses-arrested-development">https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/20...</a>) that, I guess, seeks to diminish the overall quality of Hesse's work, or otherwise to shit on ageing readers like myself (I'm 25), with such choice quotes as "Many young men, in particular, see a glamorous reflection of themselves in the typical Hesse hero—a sensitive, brooding man who cannot find a place for himself in ordinary society," or referring to the typical Hesse hero as such, "He will rebel against conventional ideas of success and refuse to pursue any kind of career, combining downward mobility with spiritual striving." I was just about to accuse the article's writer of being an "NPC" or something (I am reading it alongside typing this), but he beat me to it: "But Hesse’s heroes are punk Peter Pans—they don’t grow up, and despise people who do, because they see maturation as a surrender to conformity and accommodation." Writing ironically to contrast Hegel's idea of a "beautiful soul," writer Adam Kirsch says this: "Hesse, by analogy, might be called an ugly soul, one who is so occupied with his own spiritual distempers that the outside world barely makes an impression. This is also a key to Hesse’s appeal to young readers, who seldom see beyond the limits of the self." I cannot accept Kirsch's attempt to scathe me, for I agree almost wholeheartedly; I am too self-absorbed to care much for anything beyond the limits of my own psyche. I read Hesse for similar reasons. Dazai's <i>No Longer Human</i> is a favorite novel of mine, also for similar reasons.<br /><br />Allow me, now, to whack off over Hans's suicidal ideation:<br />"In these desperate and forlorn straits, another ghost approached the sickly boy in the guise of a treacherous comforter that gradually became familiar and indispensable: the thought of death. It was easy enough to obtain a gun or to attach a noose to a tree somewhere in the forest. The thought of death accompanied him on his daily walks. He inspected various quiet, lonely places until he finally chose one where it would be good to die. He designated this as the place where he would definitely end his life. He visited it time and again, and sitting there derived peculiar pleasure from imagining how they would soon find his corpse there. He not only chose a branch for the rope but had tested it--no further obstacles stood in his way. Little by little he composed a brief farewell letter to his father and a much longer one to Hermann Heilner. They were to be found on his corpse.<br />"These preparations with their sense of purposefulness exerted a beneficial influence on his state of mind. Sitting under the fateful branch, he enjoyed many hours during which the pressure lifted from him and a feeling of almost joyous well-being overcame him.<br />"He did not really know why he hadn't hanged himself long ago. His mind was made up, he had passed the death sentence on himself, and this made him feel so well that in the meantime he did not scorn--in these his last days--the enjoyment of sunshine and his solitary dreams in the way you do before setting out on a long trip. He could leave any day he chose, everything was settled. And he took particular and bitter satisfaction in lingering voluntarily for a while in his old surroundings, looking into the faces of people who had no idea of his dangerous resolve. Whenever he encountered the doctor, he could not help thinking: 'Well, my friend, I'd almost like to be around to see the face you'll make.'<br />"Fate allowed him to enjoy his gloomy intentions. She watched him every day sipping a few drops of joy and zest from the cup of death. There might be precious little in store for this crippled young being, but nonetheless it must complete its appointed course and not leave this earth before having drunk a little deeper of life's bitter-sweet waters." (146-147)<br />Aaaaagh, I'm gonna cooooooooooom!<br /><br />Anyway, I started laughing to myself at work because, apparently, six years ago, when I last read this novel, I'd written on page 171, alongside the paragraph of Hans coming to fall in love with the older Emma, the words "smh this n[*]gga thirsty af" [I'm censoring it in case Goodreads gets mad at me].</span>
  <a data-text-id="9745755859030841195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_859401096'><a id="like_count_review_859401096" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/859401096?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F25905.Beneath_the_Wheel">Like</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17616662206291013421">This was Hesse's first major novel and in many ways sets up his most important themes. The fact that people see criticisms of today's schooling system in America in this book, actually written about a far different educational system, speaks in some ways to the universality of its message but it also speaks to its fundamental shallowness. "Glass Bead Game" and to some extent "Narcissus and Goldmund" have much better presentations of these themes. Then again, maybe if I read this one first, I wou</span>
  <span id="freeText17616662206291013421" style="display:none">This was Hesse's first major novel and in many ways sets up his most important themes. The fact that people see criticisms of today's schooling system in America in this book, actually written about a far different educational system, speaks in some ways to the universality of its message but it also speaks to its fundamental shallowness. "Glass Bead Game" and to some extent "Narcissus and Goldmund" have much better presentations of these themes. Then again, maybe if I read this one first, I wouldn't have liked those other two nearly as much. Who knows.</span>

  <span id="freeText14785563905048517450" style="display:none">This was the first book peeking out of the secondhand cart of the Strand bookstore, and I thought what a coincidence since my bf and I have been talking about Hesse recently.<br /><br />I attribute not being impressed by this book as I was by Narcissus and Goldmund to two reasons: (A) I was reading this at the wrong time in my life; (B) I had recently read too many coming of age novels of boyhood and intellectual growth in college (E M Forster’s Maurice, Somerset Maugham’s Of Human Bondage) for this plot device to feel tiresome, and I didn’t think Hesse offered a unique perspective compared to the other two.<br /><br />The joy of reading an xth-hand book is you can peek into the excerpts that previous readers related to:<br />“Now the overworked little horse lay by the wayside, no longer of any use.”</span>
